of B r a z i l .\n\nwas s e l e c t e d by N S as a n example of a developing nation.)\nAA\n\n(Brazil\n\nW examined\ne\n\nB r a z i l i a n i n d u s t r y , government, and s o c i a l s t r u c t u technology.\nW then sought t o match t h e B r a z i l i a n economy\'s needs and t h e documented\ne\ntechnology i n t h e N S information system.\nAA\n\nF i n a l l y , we considered a l t e r mechanisms.\n\nMore s p e c i f i c a l l y ,\n\nwe found t h a t :\n\n1) Using the access provided by NASA-sponsored r e g i o n a l dissemination\nc e n t e r s , many r e p o r t s , a r t i c l e s f e r ; even where s p e c i f i c p l a u s i b l e\nmatches can be i d e n t i f i e d , they r e p r e s e n t o n l y a s t a r t i n g p o i n t\nA t every s t a g e of t h e t r a n s f e following quotation:\n\nThis p l i g h t has been expressed e f f e c t i v e l y\n\n(McClelland, 1961)\n\nNowhere does such a conclusion [ \' t h e simultaneous i r r a t i o n a l\ne f f o r t s of cannot be u t i l i z e d because of l a c k of c a p i t a l t o buy\nthem o r t h e human o r o t h e r resources necessary t o use them once\nbought.\n\nMoreover, population i s i n c r e a s i n g would i n v e s t i n an underdeveloped country.\nThe t r a n s f e r of advanced technology as a r e l e v a n t and a p p l i c a b l e resource\n\nf o r developing c o u n t r i e s appears t o provide new o p p o r t u n i t i e s .\n\nThe t a s k seems\n\nsimple enough a t f i r s t , b u t t h e formidable o b s t a c l e s of i l l i t e r a c y , a n c i e n t\nc u l t u r e s , and o f n a t i o n a l and i n t e r n a t i o n a l t e l e g r a p h network, t e l e v i s i o n and\nradio-telephone.\nP r i o r t o 1961, t h e telecommunications system w a s set up and operated t h e n a t i o n a l l e v e l , progress\nhas been slow; on t h e l o c a l l e v e l , e s i s t i n g f a c i l i t i e s are e s p e c i a l l y poor.\nB r a z i l ranks 38th i n t h e world i n t h e number of telephones per c a p i t a , and t h e\nequipment could be improved.\nThe p r e s e n t i n t e r s t a t e t o l l p l a n t used high frequency r a d i o (HFR) f o r\nt h e b a s i c system, and s i g n a l propagation can be adversely a f f e c t e d by ionos p h e r i c conditions.\n\nHowever, HFR i s r e q u i r e d i n B r a z i l f o r s e v e r a l reasons.\n\nF i r s t , t h e r e i s a l a c k of adequate e l e c t r i c power between c e n t e r s of popula-\n\nt i o n i n t h e East t o support o t h e r more r e l i a b l e systems (such as microwave);\nsecond, communication t o t h e s p a r s e l y populated n o r t h e r n and western s e c t i o n s\nof t h e country i s economic only with HFR.\n\nAll HFR l i n k s belong t o Radional\n\n(Companhia Radio I n t e r n a c i o n a l do B r a s i l ) and DCT (Departemento dos Correios e\nT e l e g r a f o s ) , and are manually switched f o r t h e most p a r t through t h e Rio de\nJaneiro office.\n\nChannel c a p a c i t y between Rio and t h e state c a p i t a l s ranges\n\nfrom one channel t o San Luis t o f i v e f o r Recife.\nI n c o n t r a s t t o t h e few channels required f o r communication between s t a t e\nc a p i t a l s i n t h e n o r t h e r n and western s e c t i o n s of t h e country, requirements\nbetween p o i n t s i n t h e s o u t h e a s t s e c t o r a r e extremely heavy.\n\nFor example,\n\nt h e states of Sao Paulo and Rio de J a n e i r o , while making up less than 5%\nof t h e land area, r e q u i r e over 50% of t h e t o t a l 1.3 m i l l i o n telephones now\n\n113\n\nin use in the country. The Southeast also differs from the North in that\nmost of its toll offices use automatic equipment. However, dial call service\nin the Southeast is very poor.\nhigh as\n\nlo%, while\n\n"Path busy" responses from local offices run as\n\n"local line busy" can run as high as 30%; thus, the percent\n\nof calls completed is very low.\n\nTo increase the capacity and reliability of the intercity toll network,\nBrazil will gradually have to replace the existing HFR system with microwave.\nThis could be done by expanding from the Rio-Sao Paulo area northward along\nthe coast and south to Porto Alegre. With this expanded system, one wideband\nchannel could also be devoted to educational TV, thereby allowing live broadcasting and network TV programs.\nFor the international telecommunications system, a satellite earth\nterminal is currently being installed outside Rio and will be in operation\nby the end of the year.\n\nThis terminal will be capable of two-way telephone,\n\nteletype, and data transmission between North America, Europe, and South America\nand will have an equivalent capacity of 12 simultaneous telephone channels.\nAt present, the HFR system includes 35 international circuits; the largest single\nrequirement is New York, with 11 circuits. The other 24 circuits are allocated\namong 16 other nations.\nTelegraph is handled in Brazil by three companies: DCT, Western Telegraph,\nand Radional. Traffic between all major cities along the East Coast from Belem\nto Porto Alegre is by undersea cable, and connections to interior points are\nover the DCT radio relay. Telex and international circuits are available in all\nprincipal cities of Brazil.\nTelevision in Brazil reaches roughly 20 million people\nof the total population.\n\n--\n\nless than 25%\n\nBy the end of 1965 there were four million TV sets\n\nin Brazil, or five per hundred inhabitants. Although the country has approximately\n60 television broadcasting stations, there is only one intercity transmission\n\nlink\n\n--\n\nbetween Rio and Brasilia. Very few cities have more than one station.\n\nSao Paulo has seven TV channels, while Rio and Porto Alegre have four each.\nThe government has reserved two UHF channels for educational TV for each of\nthe capitals of the states and territories.\nIn short, the present communication system in Brazil could be improved\nto support the economic and population expansion currently taking place in that\n\n114\n\nI has been estimated that there is a present shortage of at least\nt\n\ncountry.\n\none million telephones and that, to meet the full demands of the country over\nthe next decade, the total telephone system must grow at an annual rate of 10%\nper year.\nBrazil\'s telecommunications industry is presently represented by six major\nmanufacturing companies. The personnel in these companies are capable of\nproducing the complex equipment necessary to meet the requirements of the\ncountry. With expansion of the existing facilities in these companies, the\ndomestic needs in the telecommunication sector could be met by Brazilian\nenterprises. This, however, would create a need for more technically trained\npeople to maintain and operate the expanded and improved system.\nIn addition to the needs mentioned above, the following are some of the\nareas in which the Brazilian telecommunications industry could be helped by\n-advanced technology:\n\n.\n\nCommunications systems (including communicating with microwaves,\nmicrowave repeaters, tropospheric communications, and power\nsupply systems),\n\n.\n\nSatellite communications (including radio and aeronautical\ncommunication), and\n\n.\n\nImproved communications equipment (including unattended power\nplants, silicon integrated circuits, multiplexing techniques, and\ndevelopment of switching stations).\n\n&\n\nTRANSPORTATION\nBecause of its enormous size, great distance from most of its trading\n\npartners, and wide geographical distribution of natural resources, transportation is one of the most important elements in Brazil\'s economic welfare.\nConsequently, the lack of coordinated and efficient transportation systems\nhas been one of the biggest impediments to the development of the country\'s\neconomy. High transportation costs have been largely responsible for\npreventing Brazilian raw materials and industrial goods from being competitive\nin world markets; delays caused by outdated transporta\'ion\n\nmedia have led\n\nto the deterioration of agricultural products before they reach the consumer;\nand insufficient connections between points within the interior have kept this\nvast country from utilizing the economies of scale of its resources. Except\n115\n\nfor aviation, until now restricted to passenger traffic, Brazil does not have\na nationwide transport network.\nThe high cost of transportation and the heavy reliance on trucking in\nBrazil have been major factors in the location of industries, especially those\nwith a heavy transport commitment.\n\nInadequate transportation has served as a\n\nform of protection for the less developed regions of Brazil (such as the\nNortheast) by encouraging local development of light industries catering\nessentially to regional markets , and high transportation costs (especially in\nthe case of industries whose economic operation requires wide markets) have\nencouraged centralization within the Rio de Janeiro\n\n-\n\nSao Paulo\n\n- Belo\n\nHorizonte triangle.\nBrazil\'s present transportation networks are still strongly rooted to\nthe country\'s early history. As a result of geographical factors, the\nBrazilian coast became densely populated and developed extensive ocean\ncommunications while the interior remained lightly populated and lacked\nadequate transportation. Water transport provided the principal means of\nmoving men and goods ehrough the Brazilian interior and along the coast between\nisolated beachhead communities. Roads and railways fanned out from the coastal\ncities to the interior with few interconnections. Most railroads and early roads\nin Brazil were developed to channel raw materials, such as coffee, sugar, and\ncocoa, from the hinterland to the nearest port. As the areas of cultivation\nshifted, the railway lines were kept in operation, notwithstanding their\nrevenue losses.\na\n.\n\nRailroads and Highways\n\nBrazil does not have a national rail network, but rather five different\nsystems generally extending from the coastal regions into the interior. Although\n90% of the track is standard one-meter gauge, four other gauges are in service\n\nas well.\n\nThe railroad\'s lack of efficiency keeps it operating at a deficit, and\n\nits inadequate service affects almost all forms of commerce. A part of the\nrailroad\'s problems is rooted in a freewheeling past, when governments encouraged\nrail construction by giving a federal bonus for each mile completed. The consequence of this incentive was that the railroad network was not laid out as\nefficiently as possible.\n\nFor example, the road distance between Sao Paulo\n\nand Porto Alegre, capital of the state of Rio Grande do Sul, is about 1000 miles;\n\n116\n\nt h e d i s t a n c e by rail is 1600 m i l e s .\n\nCoupled w i t h o t h e r d e f i c i e n c i e s , t h i s\n\nmeans t h a t cargo by r a i l may t a k e as much as 15 days between t h e two cities\ncompared w i t h as l i t t l e as 48 hours by road.\nEven w i t h t h e excess d i s t a n c e s connecting major cities, Brazil\'s t o t a l\nr a i l r o a d mileage i s f a r below what one would expect i n a country i t s s i z e :\n\nI\n\nalthough i t has f i f t e e n times t h e area of France, Brazil i s served by roughly\nt h e same r a i l r o a d mileage.\n\nMoreover, almost h a l f t h e country\'s e n t i r e r a i l\n\nmileage i s i n t h e t h r e e states of Sao Paulo, Guanabra, and Minas Gerais.\nSince 1960 t h e r e h a s been a s h i f t away from r a i l r o a d s and c o a s t a l\nshipping t o highway t r a n s p o r t .
